EXPLORATION OF A FIVE-DIMENSIONAL KERR METRIC 
 
 
 
Mechid Paiman | S194730 
Christoph KГ¶hn | Supervisor 
5th July 2024 
 
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering 
Department of Space Research and Technology 
Technical University of Denmark 
 
 
==PAGE:2==
2 
This study derives a five-dimensional Kerr metric for rotating black holes, incorporating 
an additional temporal dimension. We find a partial solution based on previous 
approaches for solving the Einstein equation for rotating bodies in vacuum applicable 
to the space-time near the center of a black hole, which includes the approximate five-
dimensional Schwarzschild solution near the black holeвЂ™s center. This introduces 
additional complexity but demonstrates some consistency between the five-
dimensional Schwarzschild and five-dimensional Kerr metrics. Our findings suggest 
that addressing singularities in higher dimensions remains challenging, and future 
research into black hole behavior in extended space-time frameworks is necessary, 
potentially leading to observable predictions near the event horizon.

==PAGE:5==
 
 
INTRODUCTION 
5 
 
It has been a long journey from the nascent stages of our understanding of the universe 
to where we are now, uncovering countless mysteries along the way. We started from a 
position of knowing almost nothing and have gradually taught ourselves remarkable 
things like navigating using the stars, harnessing elements, and developing tools, 
machines, and technologies. We formulated mathematics and philosophy, intertwining 
them with physics, and eventually, we ventured beyond our Earth to discover incredible 
things about our universe: its vastness, its myriad contents, its history, and its potential 
future. This progress is truly mind-boggling. 
Our knowledge has expanded in both directions: delving into much smaller scales such 
as atoms and nanoscience, while also exploring much larger entities like stars, galaxies, 
and black holes. Significant progress has been made in physics over the past century, 
with scientists worldwide contributing to our understanding and pushing the 
boundaries of knowledge. Among the many remarkable scientists, Stephen Hawking 
stands out in the newest generations. His groundbreaking ideas have transformed our 
understanding of black holes, including his theory of Hawking radiation, which predicts 
that black holes emit radiation due to quantum effects near their event horizons, 
eventually leading to their evaporation [1]. This idea was revolutionary, challenging the 
prevailing notion that black holes were inescapable sinks of matter and energy. Hawking 
also introduced the information paradox, a profound issue arising from the apparent 
loss of information in black holes, which contradicts the principles of quantum 
mechanics [2]. This paradox has fueled ongoing debates and research, pushing the 
boundaries of our understanding of the fundamental laws of physics [3]. 
Black holes are regions in space where gravity is so strong that nothing can escape from 
them, making them invisible to direct observation. However, in 2019, the Event Horizon 
Telescope Collaboration captured the first direct image of a black holeвЂ™s shadow and 
surrounding accretion disk (Figure 1), marking a significant milestone in astrophysics 
[4]. This historic image was of the supermassive black hole at the center of the M87 
galaxy, and shortly afterward, an image of our own galaxyвЂ™s central black hole, 
Sagittarius A*, was released [5]. These cosmic giants are formed from the remnants of 
massive stars that have ended their life cycles, collapsing under their own gravity [6]. 
They are extraordinary in many ways, including their immense mass, which can reach 
millions of times the mass of our Sun [7]. These spherical bodies are characterized by 
their mass, angular momentum, and charge, as described by the вЂњno-hair theoremвЂќ 
which posits that these three characteristics are all that is needed to define them [8]. 
The nearest known black hole, Sgr A*, is located in the center of our Milky Way, about 
26 thousand light-years away from our solar system [9]. There are estimated to be 
millions of black holes in our galaxy alone [10]. 
==PAGE:6==
ROTATING BLACK HOLES IN TWO TIME DIMENSIONS 
6 
a) 
  b) 
 
Figure 1: First image of any black hole a) M87* (Messier 87) [4] and b) Sgr A* (Milky 
Way) [5] by the Event Horizon Telescope Collaboration. 
Black holes exhibit unique properties: they draw in all matter and energy within their 
event horizon, and even influence regions beyond it. This gravitational pull creates an 
area where the conventional laws of physics collapse into a singularity. This singularity 
is a point where densities become infinite and known physical laws cease to apply, 
posing significant challenges both theoretically and observationally. In 1965 Roger 
Penrose demonstrated in a first black hole singularity theorem that singularity formation 
was inevitable because of gravitational collapse [11]. This was, however, later 
questioned by Kerr [12]. Most black holes are rotating, a characteristic that profoundly 
affects their structure and the surrounding space-time [6]. The questions about where 
a black hole begins and ends, how it forms and dies, and what happens to the matter it 
absorbs, are central to our understanding of these enigmatic objects. 
These questions intersect with the theory of relativity, one of the most paradigm-shifting 
discoveries of the 20th century, credited to Albert Einstein. EinsteinвЂ™s theory of relativity, 
formulated in 1915 [13], revolutionized our understanding of space and time by unifying 
the three spatial dimensions with the temporal dimension, fundamentally altering our 
perception of the universe. This concept, while challenging to grasp, has been proven 
and reaffirmed by numerous experiments since its inception. The first proof came with 
the observation of light bending around the Sun during the solar eclipse of 1919, 
confirming EinsteinвЂ™s predictions [14]. Today, relativity is integral to various 
technologies and theoretical frameworks, although we still do not fully comprehend its 
implications. 
To explore and comprehend relativity and this novel perspective of the universe, we 
employ metrics such as the Schwarzschild [15], Kerr [16], Reissner-Weyl-NordstrГ¶m-
Jeffery [17, 18, 19, 20], and Kerr-Newman [21] metrics, each corresponding to different 
types of black holes. These provide a mathematical framework to describe the 
curvature of space-time around these massive objects [22]. The Schwarzschild metric, 
discovered by Karl Schwarzschild shortly after Einstein published his field equations, 
==PAGE:7==
INTRODUCTION 
7 
describes the space-time around a spherical, non-rotating, and non-charged massive 
body, such as a black hole without angular momentum [15]. The Kerr metric, formulated 
by Roy Kerr in 1963, extends this description to include rotating black holes, which are 
more representative of actual astrophysical black holes [16]. Additionally, other metrics 
account for various combinations of charge and rotation, providing a comprehensive 
understanding of the different types of black holes that exist. 
Multidimensional theories are also of interest in the pursuit of a unifying theory of 
everything, that can resolve singularities, bridge quantum mechanics with relativity, and 
address other gaps in our understanding of physics. Historically, higher dimensions 
have been proposed to deepen our understanding of the universe. Today, theories like 
string theory suggest the existence of extra dimensions beyond our conventional three-
dimensional space [23]. Even if these theories do not hold up, we still вЂњlearn about black 
holes and general relativity in four dimensions by studying what happens in other 
dimensions.вЂќ [24]. 
In 1986, Myers and Perry found that in more than four dimensions, black hole solutions 
resemble the familiar Schwarzschild, Reissner-NordstrГ¶m, and Kerr black holes, but 
exhibit unique features, particularly when рќ‘›> 5. For instance, they found that black 
holes can have extremely large angular momenta for a fixed mass. Moreover, in even-
dimensional space-times, black holes with negative mass, which involve causality 
violations, can also exist. Event horizons in higher dimensions can be more intricate, 
allowing for fancy configurations such as black вЂњstringsвЂќ or other unusual topologies 
[25]. Myers and Perry also proposed a potential connection between string theory and 
uncharged spinning black holes [26]. 
Emparan and Reall demonstrated that in five dimensions, an object with high angular 
momentum transitions into a вЂњblack ringвЂќ solution, characterized by an event horizon 
with the unique topology рќ‘†1 Г— рќ‘†2, challenging the previously accepted notion of black 
hole uniqueness. While it was surprising at first to find that black hole uniqueness fails 
in higher dimensions, this only emphasizes how unusual it is that such uniqueness 
holds true in four dimensions [27, 28]. Further work by Emparan revealed that neutral 
black rings, except for a narrow range near non-uniqueness, are generally unstable, 
which showcases their complex geometric properties and distortions [29]. 
In an interview, Emparan remarked that their efforts to understand black holes led them 
to explore infinite spatial and temporal dimensions, which ultimately simplified their 
models. He likened this process to the essence of physics as вЂњthe art of making 
approximationsвЂќ to simplify complex problems, stating that вЂњblack holes are physics at 
its limitsвЂќ [30]. 
A notable theory in five-dimensional space-time, different from the usual (3+2) 
dimensions, is the Kaluza-Klein black hole, often considered in a (4+1)-dimensional 
vacuum. This theory proposes that an additional compactified spatial dimension can 
==PAGE:8==
ROTATING BLACK HOLES IN TWO TIME DIMENSIONS 
8 
unify electromagnetism and gravity, suggesting unique properties for black holes in 
higher dimensions and providing a bridge to quantum theory  [31, 32]. 
Tegmark also investigated higher dimensions, arguing that dimensions other than (3+1) 
are вЂњdead worldsвЂќ, where stability is questionable [33]. But for microscopic scales, it 
may still hold. This is a fundamental assumption in string theory, positing that 10 or 26 
spatial dimensions exist, but only three have expanded, while the others remain 
compactified and are not observable at macroscopic scales [34]. 
Niarchos has compiled a comprehensive analysis of phase diagrams in higher 
dimensions, reasoning the importance of studying the dimensional dependencies in 
relativity theory to reveal properties unique to each dimension [35]. Notable solutions 
in higher-dimensional black hole physics, beyond the Myers-Perry black holes, Kaluza-
Klein black holes, and Emparan-Reall black rings, include the Pomeransky-Senkov  
black rings, which feature an additional angular momentum parameter [36], and the 
вЂќBlack SaturnвЂќ solutions, which consist of multi-component black holes where a 
spherical horizon is surrounded by a black ring [37]. These Black Saturn configurations 
are regular vacuum solutions that do not exist in four dimensions, illustrating the 
increased complexity of higher-dimensional black hole phenomena [24]. 
Despite the brilliance of EinsteinвЂ™s theory, there are still rich and complex gaps that 
invite further exploration, making physics an exciting and ever-evolving field. We have 
come a long way, but there is still much to discover, and the journey ahead promises to 
be fascinating. 
вЂњBlack holes are macroscopic objects with masses varying from a few solar masses to 
millions of solar masses. To the extent they may be considered as stationary and 
isolated, to that extent, they are all, every single one of them, described exactly by the 
Kerr solution. This is the only instance we have of an exact description of a macroscopic 
object. Macroscopic objects, as we see them all around us, are governed by a variety of 
forces, derived from a variety of approximations to a variety of physical theories. In 
contrast, the only elements in the construction of black holes are our basic concepts of 
space and time. They are, thus, almost by definition, the most perfect macroscopic 
objects there are in the universe. And since the general theory of relativity provides a 
single unique two-parameter family of solutions for their description, they are the 
simplest objects as well.вЂќ 
вЂ”S. Chandrasekhar [38]
==PAGE:9==
THEORY 
9 
 
Before drawing attention to the essence of this project, this chapter explains relevant 
foundational concepts in modern theoretical physics that underpin the study. It covers 
the theory of special and general relativity, the Schwarzschild metric in both four and 
five dimensions, and the known four-dimensional Kerr metric. 
Sections 2.1 and 2.2 are mainly based on the works of Landau & Lifshitz [39] and Adler 
et al. [22], unless specified otherwise, while Section 2.3 primarily draws from the work 
of Teukolsky [24], unless otherwise noted. 
 
The theory of relativity fundamentally changed our understanding of space, time, and 
gravity and вЂњis still considered to be the most important idea in modern physics. It 
rewrote Isaac NewtonвЂ™s physical laws devised 200 years earlier and created an elegant 
way to understand the UniverseвЂќ [40]. It is divided into two parts: special relativity and 
general relativity. 
Special relativity describes phenomena in a force-free space-time. It was first 
introduced by Einstein in his third seminal paper [41] from 1905, which is referred to as 
his вЂњannus mirabilisвЂќ, вЂњmiracle yearвЂќ, as he wrote four outstanding papers that 
fundamentally reshaped modern physics. This theory reveals that time and space are 
relative and intertwined, altering our perception of these dimensions. It describes the 
physics of objects moving at constant velocities, particularly those near the speed of 
light. It establishes that the laws of physics are consistent for all observers in uniform 
motion relative to one another. The concept of time dilation is introduced here, meaning 
that time slows down for objects moving at high speeds relative to an observer. 
Equivalently, length contraction is another keyword, which is when objects contract in 
the direction of motion as they approach the speed of light. 
General relativity was introduced by Einstein in 1915 and it extends the principles of 
special relativity to include acceleration and the effects of gravity [13]. Unlike the 
Newtonian view of gravity as a force between masses, general relativity describes 
gravity as the curvature of space-time itself caused by the presence of mass and energy. 
This theory explains gravitational lensing, the bending of light around massive objects, 
orbital precession, the precise motion of planets and stars due to space-time curvature, 
and black holes. EinsteinвЂ™s general relativity contrasts sharply with Newtonian physics 
by establishing that the laws of physics hold in all reference frames, whether 
accelerating or not. 
==PAGE:10==
ROTATING BLACK HOLES IN TWO TIME DIMENSIONS 
10 
Four-vectors are introduced to describe physical quantities in a four-dimensional 
space-time framework, seamlessly integrating temporal and spatial components into a 
single entity. This unified approach allows for consistent treatment of these dimensions 
and can be extended to describe other quantities such as four-velocity and four-
acceleration. The general form of a four-vector is given by:

denoted by the numerical indices 0, 1, 2, 3, respectively. 
A metric defines the geometry of space-time, and the simplest metric in relativity, i.e., 
the limit of special relativity, is the Minkowski metric for flat space-time, developed by 
Hermann Minkowski in 1908 [42], built upon EinsteinвЂ™s theory of special relativity. It is:

This metric treats time and space on equal footing but with different signs, ensuring the 
equivalency between a point in space and the time of the event occurring at that point. 
If all coordinates had the same sign, this homogeneity would be disrupted. Similarly, if 
the line element depended on the direction of the relative velocity, it would violate the 
isotropy of space. Whether time is positive and space negative, or vice versa, is just 
convention. The upper- and lower-case indices represent different forms of a tensor and 
relate to its symmetry. Lower-case indices, like рќњ‚рќњ‡рќњ€, denote the covariant form, while 
upper-case indices, e.g. рќњ‚рќњ‡рќњ€, represent the contravariant form, which is the inverse of 
the covariant form [39]. 
The line element is a fundamental concept in general relativity used to describe the 
infinitesimal distance between two points in space-time. It can be calculated using the 
metric tensor, which encapsulates the geometric properties of the space-time. For the 
Minkowski metric, the line element is expressed as:

The Schwarzschild metric is a key solution to EinsteinвЂ™s field equations, capturing the 
gravitational field around a stating, uncharged, spherically symmetric mass. 
Deriving the metric begins with EinsteinвЂ™s field equation, where we set the stress-energy 
tensor to zero as we are considering a vacuum region outside the mass. The 
cosmological constant О› is typically neglected due to its minimal effect in this context. 
Thus, the focus shifts to the Einstein tensor:

This metric was the first found solution to satisfy the Einstein field equation, providing a 
solution for the space-time outside of a spherically symmetric, non-charged, non-
rotating body. 
 
Paiman et al. [43] extend the traditional four-dimensional Schwarzschild solution to five 
dimensions вЂ“ three spatial and two temporal вЂ“ resulting in a higher-dimensional 
Schwarzschild metric. This extension introduces a second time-like dimension, 
necessitating the formulation of new equations and variables to accommodate the 
additional complexity. Below, we provide a summary of their findings.

The introduction of a second time dimension effectively mitigates the metric's tendency 
to diverge near the central singularity of black holes, thus resolving the singularity 
problem. Further details are provided in [43]. 
 
The Kerr metric takes it a step further and describes the geometry of space-time around 
a rotating massive body. It provides a more realistic model for astrophysical black holes, 
which are typically rotating due to the conservation of angular momentum from their 
progenitor stars. 
In mathematical terms, the Kerr metric is another solution to the Einstein field equation 
in vacuum, which assumes axial symmetry and rotation. It is characterized by two 
parameters: the mass рќ‘Ђ and the angular momentum per unit mass рќ‘Ћ=
рќђЅ
рќ‘Ђ of the rotating 
body. The metric is usually expressed in Boyer-Lindquist coordinates (рќ‘Ў, рќ‘џ, рќњѓ, рќњ‘), where 
рќ‘Ў is the time coordinate, рќ‘џ is the radial coordinate, рќњѓ is the polar angle, and рќњ‘ is the 
azimuthal angle, from their 1967 paper [44].

15

The off-diagonal term is a key difference to the Schwarzschild metric. When рќ‘Ћ= 0, the 
metric reduces to SchwarzschildвЂ™s form. This form of the Kerr metric is also singular at 
рќ‘џ2 + рќ‘Ћ2 в€’2рќ‘Ђрќ‘џ= 0, but this is a coordinate singularity, not a physical one. 
 
Figure 2: Sketch of a rotating black hole structure, showing its ring singularity, inner 
and outer horizons, and the ergosphere. 
Rotating black holes have two event horizons: an outer and an inner horizon, seen in 
Figure 2. The outer horizon acts like that of a Schwarzschild black hole, with time dilation 
becoming extreme as it is approached. Inside, between the two horizons, time and 
space get interchanged, and past the inner horizon, time accelerates towards the 
singularity [45]. These horizons are determined by: 
рќ‘џВ± = рќ‘ЂВ± в€љрќ‘Ђ2 в€’рќ‘Ћ2.
(23) 
A time Killing vector represents a symmetry in time and preserves the structure of the 
metric over time. In the Kerr metric, this vector changes from being timelike to spacelike 
in a region outside the event horizon. The boundary where the Killing vector becomes 
zero is called the ergosurface or stationary limit. The space between the event horizon 
and the ergosurface is known as the ergosphere, and the presence of the ergosphere 
allows for various energy extraction mechanisms from a rotating black hole. 
 
==PAGE:16==
ROTATING BLACK HOLES IN TWO TIME DIMENSIONS 
16 
       
Figure 3: Illustration of Penrose process 
on a rotating black hole [46]. 
Figure 4: Illustration of frame dragging 
effect around a rotating black hole [47].
In the Penrose process, illustrated in Figure 3, a particle that enters the ergosphere 
splits into two, with one part carrying negative energy and falling into the black hole, 
while the other part escapes to infinity with more energy than the original particle, thus 
reducing the black holeвЂ™s rotational energy [48]. In relation to the Penrose process, the 
ergosphere is actively dragged along with the black holeвЂ™s rotation, influencing the 
trajectories of particles and light rays, forcing them to more complex paths. This 
phenomenon is known as frame dragging, illustrated in Figure 4. [48, 49] 
 
There are many ways to derive the Kerr metric and simpler methods have been 
developed, building upon KerrвЂ™s original solution to the rotating black hole, which was 
вЂњa tour de force of complicated calculationsвЂќ [24]. We focus on a derivation presented 
by Teukolsky in his 2015 paper, вЂњThe Kerr MetricвЂќ [24]. TeukolskyвЂ™s method stands out 
for its pedagogical clarity while maintaining the necessary rigor, making it an excellent 
foundation for further exploration and extension of the Kerr metric. This derivation 
synthesizes the most effective elements from several educational approaches to create 
a coherent and understandable method. It acknowledges that some heuristic 
assumptions are necessary to begin with a metric in an appropriate form, simplifying 
the field equations to make them solvable and leading to the desired metric. The choice 
of assumptions is inherently subjective and influenced by the known solution. Our 
approach builds upon TeukolskyвЂ™s framework, introducing modifications to 
accommodate the specific requirements of our research, such as different sign 
conventions, variable naming, and coordinate adjustments.

metric tensor рќ‘”рќњ‡рќњ€. Introducing the coordinate system four-vector рќ‘Ґрќњ‡ given by:

==PAGE:21==
GENERAL FOUR-DIMENSIONAL KERR METRIC 
21 
 
Our objective is to extend the Kerr metric and derive a form suitable for a five-
dimensional framework for rotating black holes. We aim to achieve this by generalizing 
TeukolskyвЂ™s derivation of the four-dimensional Kerr metric to accommodate an 
additional dimension. In doing so, we introduce the functions рќђґ(рќ‘џ), рќ‘Ќ(рќ‘џ), and рќђµ(рќ‘џ) 
within a modified Schwarzschild metric, providing a generalized representation of the 
metric components. We use this modified Schwarzschild metric, along with the 
Minkowski metric, as baselines. By comparing and refining these baselines, we develop 
a methodology to calculate the five-dimensional Kerr metric. This approach allows us 
to incorporate approximate solutions from the five-dimensional Schwarzschild metric 
[43]. 
This procedure is analogous to the way the Kerr metric was originally derived by 
expanding upon the Schwarzschild metric in four dimensions. By inserting the 
approximate solutions for рќђґ(рќ‘џ), рќ‘Ќ(рќ‘џ), and рќђµ(рќ‘џ) from the five-dimensional 
Schwarzschild solution into our Kerr metric formulation, we successfully derive a five-
dimensional Kerr metric. We then investigate this derived 5D Kerr metric by analyzing its 
components with respect to the radial variable рќ‘џ. Due to the complexity of the metric, 
we focus on specific values of рќ‘џ to understand the behavior and properties of the metric 
in different regions of the extended space-time. 
This chapter develops a generalized four-dimensional Kerr metric based on TeukolskyвЂ™s 
derivation. We start by considering a generalized version of the line element in Equation 
(32), substituting рќђґ(рќ‘џ) for the coefficient of рќ‘‘рќ‘Ў2 and рќђµ(рќ‘џ) for the coefficient of рќ‘‘рќ‘џ2: 
рќ‘‘рќ‘ 2 = рќђґ(рќ‘џ)рќ‘‘рќ‘Ў2 в€’рќђµ(рќ‘џ)рќ‘‘рќ‘џ2 в€’рќ‘џ2рќ‘‘рќњѓ2 в€’(рќ‘џ2 + рќ‘Ћ2) sin2 рќњѓрќ‘‘рќњ‘2 в€’4рќ‘Ђрќ‘Ћ
рќ‘џ

FIVE-DIMENSIONAL KERR METRIC 
26 
 
In this chapter, we extend our space-time framework by incorporating an additional 
temporal dimension to construct a Kerr metric applicable to five dimensions. We start 
with the general case in Section 4.1, and then move to specific cases of the metric in 
Sections 4.2 and 4.3. 
 
Building on the previous chapter, we will now extend the general Kerr metric to five 
dimensions. We begin by extending the four-vector to a five-vector:

Getting to the Einstein field equation, we know from Section 2.2 that we solve the field 
equation by solving the Ricci tensor equal to zero. 
рќ‘…рќњ‡рќњ€= 0.

The Christoffel symbols are calculated using the computer program Mathematica, 
resulting in 34 non-zero components. Subsequently, the Ricci tensor is then computed 
with a focus on рќ‘…рќ‘џрќ‘џ, using the corresponding Ricci tensor formula in Equation (10). This 
leads us to a system of equations: 
рќ‘…рќ‘Ўрќ‘Ў= 0, 
рќ‘…рќњЏрќњЏ= 0, 
рќ‘…рќњѓрќњѓ= 0, 
рќ‘…рќњ‘рќњ‘= 0,
(93)
which can be found in the appendix chapter, specifically in the Mathematica input 
In[52,53,55,56], and 
рќ‘…рќ‘џрќ‘џ= О”рќ‘џрќ‘џ,6(рќ‘џ) рќ‘ћ6 + О”рќ‘џрќ‘џ,5(рќ‘џ) рќ‘ћ5 + О”рќ‘џрќ‘џ,4(рќ‘џ) рќ‘ћ4 + О”рќ‘џрќ‘џ,3(рќ‘џ) рќ‘ћ3 + О”рќ‘џрќ‘џ,2(рќ‘џ) рќ‘ћ2
+О”рќ‘џрќ‘џ,1(рќ‘џ) рќ‘ћ+ О”рќ‘џрќ‘џ,0(рќ‘џ) = 0,
(94) 
where the coefficients О”рќ‘џрќ‘џ,6(рќ‘џ), вЂ¦ , О”рќ‘џрќ‘џ,0(рќ‘џ) are also provided in the appendix in Out[57-
63]. Due to the principle of independence, this essentially gives us 32 equations that 
must be satisfied by solutions for рќ‘Њ(рќ‘џ), рќ‘‹(рќ‘џ), and рќђ№(рќ‘џ), along with the insertion of рќђґ(рќ‘џ) 
(19) and рќ‘Ќ(рќ‘џ) (20). The resulting system is extraordinarily complex and necessitates 
simplification. Therefore, we consider a more specific scenario in the following section, 
focusing on conditions closer to the center of the massive body. 
 
A way to simplify the metric is to focus on regions close to the center of the black hole, 
specifically where рќ‘џв‰Єрќ‘Ћ. For instance, for Sgr A*, the angular momentum per unit mass 
рќ‘Ћ is given by the dimensionless (normalized) spin parameter рќ‘Ћв€—= 0.9 [50]: 
рќ‘Ћ= рќ‘Ћв€—рќ‘Ђ= 7.7 в€™1036.
(95) 
In this scenario, рќ‘џ is negligible compared to рќ‘Ћ, indicating рќ‘џв‰Єрќ‘Ћ. Consequently, in 
situations where these variables are interrelated by summation, we can effectively 
simplify the metric by making the following assumption: 
рќњЊ2 = рќ‘џ2 + рќ‘Ћ2рќ‘ћв†’рќ‘Ћ2рќ‘ћ,
(96) 
рќ‘џ2 + рќ‘Ћ2 в†’рќ‘Ћ2.
(97) 
Note that we do not automatically set рќ‘џ= 0, but rather only in the specific case 
mentioned in (96,97) above. 
For the sake of consistency in nomenclature, we denote the unknowns as 
рќ‘Њ0(рќ‘џ), рќ‘‹0(рќ‘џ), рќђ№0(рќ‘џ), signifying that these variables specifically pertain to the simplified 
metric near рќ‘џ= 0. This simplified metric is also referred to as рќ‘”0 рќњ‡рќњ€.

DISCUSSION 
37 
 
In Equations (100,101) and the appendix (Out[106-108]), we successfully derive 
expressions for рќ‘Њ0(рќ‘џ), рќ‘‹0(рќ‘џ), and рќђ№0(рќ‘џ), leading to the metric рќ‘”0 рќњ‡рќњ€, which describes the 
space-time near the center of a black hole, based on TeukolskyвЂ™s four-dimensional Kerr 
derivation [24]. This metric incorporates functions рќђґ(рќ‘џ) and рќ‘Ќ(рќ‘џ) from the five-
dimensional Schwarzschild approximation close to the center. Since this solution is 
complex, verifying it against the remaining components of the Einstein field equations 
is a challenging task. The expressions for рќ‘Њ0(рќ‘џ), рќ‘‹0(рќ‘џ), and рќђ№0(рќ‘џ) must satisfy the entire 
system of equations (93,94) that arise from the principle of independence used in our 
method. Although the equations are long and intricate, obtaining a symbolic solution is 
a promising first step. 
Alternative solutions exist, but these often lead to mathematical singularities that are 
not physically meaningful, or they are too complex for practical application. The 
solvability here is particularly challenging due to the sheer size and complexity of the 
equations, even for computational tools. Simplifying the equations could be a viable 
approach to managing this complexity. 
In Figure 6, for moderate to large values of рќђ¶1 (1 and 105), the 5D Kerr coefficient for the 
рќ‘Ў component grows exponentially, reaching magnitudes of 1070 and 1040 at рќ‘џв‰€10в€’40. 
Figure 7 shows the corresponding component in the 5D Schwarzschild metric, which 
also increases significantly to approximately 1050, aligning roughly in order of 
magnitude with our 5D Kerr metric. Stability is demonstrated at high рќђ¶1 values in Figure 
7, which helps mitigate singularities. The plots in Figure 6 for the 5D Kerr metric also 
show less explosive behavior than anticipated, particularly at higher рќђ¶1 values, 
suggesting potential singularity resolution. 
Interestingly, the second time component рќњЏ mirrors the first time component рќ‘Ў, leading 
one to assume similar metric components. However, this is not the case, as the 
component for time рќ‘Ў (shown in Figure 6) is an exponentially dependent function of рќ‘џ, 
while the component for time рќњЏ remains constant, as seen in Equation (117). This 
discrepancy might be due to the fact that the second time operates on a small space 
compared to the first time. 
It is also crucial to verify whether our solution reduces to the four-dimensional Kerr 
metric by eliminating рќ‘Ќ(рќ‘џ) and the second temporal coordinate рќњЏ. This step is difficult 
right now due to the large expressions involved, but it may be validated numerically. 
Ideally, obtaining a solution for the most general metric рќ‘”рќњ‡рќњ€ would be the ultimate goal, 
but the complexity of the computations makes this an area for future research. Having 
developed a simpler form of the metric is a significant first step towards potentially 
solving for the most general case. 
==PAGE:38==
ROTATING BLACK HOLES IN TWO TIME DIMENSIONS 
38 
Comparing the five-dimensional solution to the Kerr metric with the four-dimensional 
solution reveals the added complexity introduced by the additional temporal 
dimension. Incorporating the five-dimensional Schwarzschild approximation into our 
Kerr solution considerably expands the expressions, indicating that even microscopic 
temporal dimensions profoundly impact the metric. Handling such small values and 
distances is challenging, which explains why analyses usually do not study so deeply 
into the center or consider additional time dimensions. Despite the lack of elegance in 
this approach, it offers substantial insights into black holes and higher dimensions. 
Furthermore, this work contributes significantly to the broader effort to describe a black 
hole's interior when considering two temporal dimensions. It aids in the interpretation 
of black hole singularities within extended space-time frameworks and potentially 
enables the formulation of observable predictions near the event horizon.
==PAGE:39==
 
 
CONCLUSION 
39 
 
In this study, we have derived a five-dimensional Kerr metric under the condition рќ‘џв‰Єрќ‘Ћ, 
where рќ‘Ћ represents the angular momentum per unit mass of the black hole. This metric 
is applicable to the region near the center of a rotating black hole and provides a partial 
solution to the Einstein field equations in a five-dimensional space-time framework, 
incorporating a second temporal dimension. While our solution offers promising 
results, it requires thorough verification against all components of the Einstein field 
equations to ensure its validity. 
The metric we have obtained describes a five-dimensional world where, alongside the 
traditional three spatial dimensions and one temporal dimension, there is an additional 
compactified temporal dimension, рќњЏ, acting on a small space, analogous to concepts 
found in string theory. This approach introduces a (3+2)-dimensional space-time and 
extends our understanding beyond the conventional (3+1) dimensions. 
Upon analyzing a specific example of a black hole, we have found that our metric's 
components align with the orders of magnitude of the five-dimensional Schwarzschild 
metric [43]. Our derived partial solution introduces complexities and simplifying it may 
be necessary for practical application. Notably, high values of рќђ¶1 have demonstrated 
less instability in our 5D metric components, potentially mitigating singularities 
observed at lower radii, although exponential growth was still noted at these scales. 
We have observed that the second temporal component, рќњЏ, differs significantly from the 
first, рќ‘Ў, indicating that they operate over different spatial influences. This highlights the 
unique challenges presented by additional temporal dimensions in our extended 
space-time model. 
Our approach suggests that the derived metric could be reduced to the four-
dimensional Kerr solution by eliminating the additional dimension, though this step 
remains to be validated numerically due to the complexity of the expressions involved. 
Furthermore, the study underscores the substantial impact that even microscopic 
temporal dimensions have on the metric, pointing to the complexity of handling such 
dimensions. 
The physical implications are significant, as understanding and resolving singularities in 
higher-dimensional spaces, particularly those with two temporal dimensions, remains 
a crucial yet complex task. This work contributes to the broader effort to describe black 
hole interiors within extended space-time frameworks and aids in potentially 
formulating observable predictions near event horizons. Our findings indicate that there 
is still much to explore in the field of higher-dimensional metrics. 
==PAGE:40==
 
 
FUTURE WORK 
40 
 
This research advances the broader understanding of rotating black holes in extended 
space-time dimensions. While our specific partial solution provides promising insights, 
several areas require further exploration to enhance its applicability and accuracy. 
First, it is crucial to verify whether our solution to the specific case of the metric near the 
center satisfies the entire system of equations (93,94). Notably, setting the 
accompanying functions of рќ‘‘рќњЏ equal to zero should theoretically reduce our model to 
its four-dimensional counterpart, yet this is not the case. However, if we nullify the 
entire terms containing рќ‘Ќ(рќ‘џ) and рќ‘‹(рќ‘џ), the model does indeed converge to the four-
dimensional version. Additionally, exploring a wider range of values for the constant рќђ¶1 
and potentially other constants could reveal new dynamics within the metric. Analyzing 
how different values influence the solution may uncover hidden properties and offer a 
more comprehensive understanding. Future research could also focus on finding a 
metric solution at рќ‘џ= рќ‘џрќ‘†, as discussed in Section 4.3. Additionally, advancing towards 
a solution for the most general form of the metric, as outlined in Metric (92), will be a 
significant milestone. 
A valuable extension of this work involves simulating the particle motion in a given 
metric to visualize how particles behave in the vicinity of a black hole within this 
framework. Such simulations could provide tangible insights into the behavior of matter 
and energy under extreme gravitational conditions. On a more ambitious scale, one 
could explore metrics in systems with an undefined number of spatial and temporal 
dimensions. This endeavor could lead to groundbreaking discoveries in higher-
dimensional space-time. Any form of further investigation can help to interpret black 
hole singularities within extended space-time frameworks and help describe black 
holes' interiors with two time dimensions, potentially making observable predictions 
near the event horizon. Such work is essential for deepening our understanding of black 
holes and their complex structures. 
Finally, the Kerr metric serves as a critical foundation for investigating more intricate 
phenomena in the universe, including the behavior of accretion disks, relativistic jets, 
and the dynamics of matter in extreme gravitational fields. This research not only 
enhances our comprehension of black holes but also opens the door for future 
explorations into the fascinating and complex nature of our universe.

Calculating and displaying the Riemann tensor
The components of the Riemann tensor, RО»ОјОЅПѓ, are calculated using the definition given above.
In[10]:= riemann := riemann = Simplify[Table[
D[christoffelгЂљi, j, lгЂ›, coordгЂљkгЂ›] - D[christoffelгЂљi, j, kгЂ›, coordгЂљlгЂ›] +
Sum[
christoffelгЂљs, j, lгЂ›Г—christoffelгЂљi, k, sгЂ›- christoffelгЂљs, j, kгЂ›Г—christoffelгЂљi, l, sгЂ›,
{s, 1, n}],
{i, 1, n}, {j, 1, n}, {k, 1, n}, {l, 1, n}] ]

==PAGE:90==
Low value of C1
In this section, we evaluate the metric components g0 Ој ОЅ at discrete values of r  for the case study of Sgr 
A*. Given the complexity and size of the expressions for the metric components, we focus on specific 
points rather than continuous plotting.
To explore the influence of the constant C1 on the metric, we compute this for three different values: 
C1 = 10-11 (a very low value), C1 = 1 (a moderate value), and C1 = 105 (a very high value).
The range of r used is rRange for all plots. However, specifically for the low value of C1, we extend our 
investigation to a higher range of r , denoted as rHigh.
The ranges of r and the constants are defined as follows:
In[111]:=
rHigh = Range[0.05, 5, 0.1];
rRange = 10^Range[0, -40, -5];
In[113]:=
G = 1; (* N m^2 kg^-2 *)
c = 1; (* m/s *)
M = 8.54*10^36;(* kg *)
rS = 2*G*M/c^2 (* m *)
K2 = 10^-20;
a = 0.9*M
q = 0.5;
C1 = 10^-11;
C2 = Chop[K2/(-rS)^C1]
О» = Simplify[Sqrt[1 + C1 + C1^2]]
m = Simplify[1 + C1 + О»]
